<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Taste</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">This article is about the sense. For the culinary term, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tasting" title="Tasting">Tasting</a>. For the social and aesthetic aspects of "taste", see <a href="/wiki/Taste_(sociology)" title="Taste (sociology)">Taste (sociology)</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Taste_(disambiguation)" title="Taste (disambiguation)">Taste (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taste_bud.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="842" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Taste_bud.svg/220px-Taste_bud.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Taste_bud.svg/330px-Taste_bud.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Taste_bud.svg/440px-Taste_bud.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taste_bud.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Taste bud</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Taste</b>, <b>gustatory perception</b>, or <b>gustation</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is one of the five traditional <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">senses</a> that belongs to the <b>gustatory system</b>.</p>
<p>Taste is the sensation produced when a substance in the mouth <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">reacts chemically</a> with <a href="/wiki/Taste_receptor" title="Taste receptor">taste receptor</a> cells located on <a href="/wiki/Taste_bud" title="Taste bud">taste buds</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_cavity" title="Oral cavity">oral cavity</a>, mostly on the <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a>. Taste, along with smell (<a href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">olfaction</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Trigeminal_nerve" title="Trigeminal nerve">trigeminal nerve</a> stimulation (registering texture, pain, and temperature), determines <a href="/wiki/Flavor" title="Flavor">flavors</a> of <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> or other substances. Humans have taste receptors on taste buds (gustatory calyculi) and other areas including the upper surface of the <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Epiglottis" title="Epiglottis">epiglottis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kids_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-kids-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The tongue is covered with thousands of small bumps called <a href="/wiki/Lingual_papillae" title="Lingual papillae">papillae</a>, which are visible to the naked eye. Within each papilla are hundreds of taste buds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schacter169_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schacter169-4">[4]</a></sup> The exception to this is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filiform_papillae" title="Filiform papillae">filiform papillae</a> that do not contain taste buds. There are between 2000 and 5000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boron.2C_W.F._2003_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boron.2C_W.F._2003-5">[5]</a></sup> taste buds that are located on the back and front of the tongue. Others are located on the roof, sides and back of the mouth, and in the throat. Each taste bud contains 50 to 100 taste receptor cells.</p>
<p>The sensation of taste includes five established basic tastes: <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">sweetness</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sourness" title="Sourness">sourness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taste#Saltiness" title="Taste">saltiness</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitter_(taste)#Bitterness" title="Bitter (taste)">bitterness</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kean_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Scientific experiments have proven that these five tastes exist and are distinct from one another.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Taste buds are able to differentiate among different tastes through detecting interaction with different molecules or ions. Sweet, umami, and bitter tastes are triggered by the binding of molecules to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G_protein-coupled_receptors" title="G protein-coupled receptors">G protein-coupled receptors</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a> of taste buds. Saltiness and sourness are perceived when <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">ions</a> enter taste buds, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The basic tastes contribute only partially to the sensation and flavor of food in the mouth—other factors include <a href="/wiki/Odor" title="Odor">smell</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kids_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-kids-2">[2]</a></sup> detected by the <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_epithelium" title="Olfactory epithelium">olfactory epithelium</a> of the nose;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Texture_(food)" title="Texture (food)">texture</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> detected through a variety of <a href="/wiki/Mechanoreceptor" title="Mechanoreceptor">mechanoreceptors</a>, muscle nerves, etc.;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> temperature, detected by <a href="/wiki/Thermoreceptor" title="Thermoreceptor">thermoreceptors</a>; and "coolness" (such as of <a href="/wiki/Menthol" title="Menthol">menthol</a>) and "hotness" (<a href="/wiki/Pungency" title="Pungency">pungency</a>), through <a href="/wiki/Chemesthesis" title="Chemesthesis">chemesthesis</a>.</p>
<p>As taste senses both harmful and beneficial things, all basic tastes are classified as either aversive or appetitive, depending upon the effect the things they sense have on our bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aa_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-aa-12">[12]</a></sup> Sweetness helps to identify energy-rich foods, while bitterness serves as a warning sign of poisons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>, taste perception begins to fade around 50 years of age because of loss of tongue papillae and a general decrease in <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a> production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeidelBall2010_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-SeidelBall2010-14">[14]</a></sup> Also, not all <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> share the same taste senses: some <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> can taste <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> (which humans cannot), <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> cannot taste sweetness, and several other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivores" title="Carnivores">carnivores</a> including <a href="/wiki/Hyena" title="Hyena">hyenas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_lion" title="Sea lion">sea lions</a>, have lost the ability to sense up to four of their ancestral five taste senses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_tastes">Basic tastes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Basic tastes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(September 2016)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Taste in the gustatory system allows humans to distinguish between safe and harmful food, and to gauge foods’ nutritional value. <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">Digestive enzymes</a> in saliva begin to dissolve food into base chemicals that are washed over the papillae and detected as tastes by the taste buds. The tongue is covered with thousands of small bumps called <a href="/wiki/Lingual_papillae" title="Lingual papillae">papillae</a>, which are visible to the naked eye. Within each papilla are hundreds of taste buds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schacter169_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schacter169-4">[4]</a></sup> The exception to this are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filiform_papillae" title="Filiform papillae">filiform papillae</a> that do not contain taste buds. There are between 2000 and 5000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boron.2C_W.F._2003_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boron.2C_W.F._2003-5">[5]</a></sup> taste buds that are located on the back and front of the tongue. Others are located on the roof, sides and back of the mouth, and in the throat. Each taste bud contains 50 to 100 taste receptor cells.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitter_(taste)" title="Bitter (taste)">Bitter</a> foods are generally found unpleasant, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sour" title="Sour">sour</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltiness" title="Saltiness">salty</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet" title="Sweet">sweet</a>, and meaty tasting foods generally provide a pleasurable sensation. The five specific tastes received by <a href="/wiki/Taste_receptor" title="Taste receptor">taste receptors</a> are saltiness, sweetness, bitterness, sourness, and <i><a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a></i>, which means ‘delicious’ in Japanese and is sometimes translated as “savory” in English. As of the early twentieth century, physiologists and psychologists believed there were four basic tastes: sweetness, sourness, saltiness, and bitterness. At that time umami was not identified<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> but now a large number of authorities recognize it as the fifth taste.</p>
<p>According to Lindemann, both salt and sour taste mechanisms detect, in different ways, the presence of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> (salt) in the mouth, however, acids are also detected and perceived as sour.</p>
<p>The detection of salt is important to many organisms, but specifically mammals, as it serves a critical role in ion and water <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> in the body. It is specifically needed in the mammalian <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidney</a> as an osmotically active compound which facilitates passive re-uptake of water into the blood.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Which studies purport this? (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Because of this, salt elicits a pleasant taste in most humans.</p>
<p>Sour and salt tastes can be pleasant in small quantities, but in larger quantities become more and more unpleasant to taste. For sour taste this is presumably because the sour taste can signal under-ripe fruit, rotten meat, and other spoiled foods, which can be dangerous to the body because of bacteria which grow in such media. Additionally, sour taste signals <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acids" title="Acids">acids</a>, which can cause serious tissue damage.</p>
<p>The bitter taste is almost universally unpleasant to humans. This is because many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogenous" title="Nitrogenous">nitrogenous</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic molecules</a> which have a <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacological</a> effect on humans taste bitter. These include <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">nicotine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strychnine" title="Strychnine">strychnine</a>, which respectively compose the stimulant in <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>, addictive agent in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cigarettes" title="Cigarettes">cigarettes</a>, and active compound in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticides" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a>. It appears that some psychological process allows humans to overcome their innate aversion to bitter taste, as caffeinated drinks are widely consumed and enjoyed around the world. It is also interesting to note that many common medicines have a bitter taste if chewed; the gustatory system apparently interprets these compounds as poisons. In this manner, the unpleasant reaction to the bitter taste is a last-line warning system before the compound is ingested and can do damage.</p>
<p>Sweet taste signals the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> in solution. Since carbohydrates have a very high calorie count (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saccharides" title="Saccharides">saccharides</a> have many bonds, therefore much energy), they are desirable to the human body, which evolved to seek out the highest calorie intake foods. They are used as direct energy (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugars" title="Sugars">sugars</a>) and storage of energy (<a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>). However, there are many non-carbohydrate molecules that trigger a sweet response, leading to the development of many artificial sweeteners, including <a href="/wiki/Saccharin" title="Saccharin">saccharin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sucralose" title="Sucralose">sucralose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aspartame" title="Aspartame">aspartame</a>. It is still unclear how these substances activate the sweet receptors and what adaptational significance this has had.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a> taste, identified by Japanese chemist <a href="/wiki/Kikunae_Ikeda" title="Kikunae Ikeda">Kikunae Ikeda</a> of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">Tokyo Imperial University</a>, which signals the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L-glutamate" title="L-glutamate">L-glutamate</a>, triggers a pleasurable response and thus encourages the intake of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peptides" title="Peptides">peptides</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">proteins</a>. The amino acids in proteins are used in the body to build muscles and organs, transport molecules (<a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a>, and the organic catalysts known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymes" title="Enzymes">enzymes</a>. These are all critical molecules, and as such it is important to have a steady supply of amino acids, hence the pleasurable response to their presence in the mouth.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a> countries within the sphere of mainly <a href="/wiki/Chinese_cuisine" title="Chinese cuisine">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_cuisine" title="Indian cuisine">Indian</a> cultural influence, <a href="/wiki/Pungency" title="Pungency">pungency</a> (piquancy or hotness) had traditionally been considered a sixth basic taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-17">[17]</a></sup> In 2015, researchers at <a href="/wiki/Purdue_University" title="Purdue University">Purdue University</a> suggested a new basic taste (of fats) called <b>oleogustus</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oleogustus_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-oleogustus-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweetness">Sweetness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sweetness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signal_Transaction_of_the_Sweet_Taste.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="512" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Signal_Transaction_of_the_Sweet_Taste.svg/220px-Signal_Transaction_of_the_Sweet_Taste.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Signal_Transaction_of_the_Sweet_Taste.svg/330px-Signal_Transaction_of_the_Sweet_Taste.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Signal_Transaction_of_the_Sweet_Taste.svg/440px-Signal_Transaction_of_the_Sweet_Taste.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signal_Transaction_of_the_Sweet_Taste.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The diagram above depicts the signal transduction pathway of the sweet taste. Object A is a taste bud, object B is one taste cell of the taste bud, and object C is the neuron attached to the taste cell. I. Part I shows the reception of a molecule. 1. Sugar, the first messenger, binds to a protein receptor on the cell membrane. II. Part II shows the transduction of the relay molecules. 2. G Protein-coupled receptors, second messengers, are activated. 3. G Proteins activate adenylate cyclase, an enzyme, which increases the cAMP concentration. Depolarization occurs. 4. The energy, from step 3, is given to activate the K+, potassium, protein channels.III. Part III shows the response of the taste cell. 5. Ca+, calcium, protein channels is activated.6. The increased Ca+ concentration activates neurotransmitter vesicles. 7. The neuron connected to the taste bud is stimulated by the neurotransmitters.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">Sweetness</a></div>
<p>Sweetness, usually regarded as a pleasurable sensation, is produced by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a> and a few other substances. Sweetness is often connected to <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>, which contain a <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl_group" title="Carbonyl group">carbonyl group</a>. Sweetness is detected by a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G_protein_coupled_receptor" title="G protein coupled receptor">G protein coupled receptors</a> coupled to the <a href="/wiki/G_protein" title="G protein">G protein</a> <a href="/wiki/Gustducin" title="Gustducin">gustducin</a> found on the <a href="/wiki/Taste_bud" title="Taste bud">taste buds</a>. At least two different variants of the "sweetness receptors" must be activated for the brain to register sweetness. Compounds the brain senses as sweet are thus compounds that can bind with varying bond strength to two different sweetness receptors. These receptors are T1R2+3 (heterodimer) and T1R3 (homodimer), which account for all sweet sensing in humans and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Taste detection thresholds for sweet substances are rated relative to <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, which has an index of 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaughlin.26Margolskee_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLaughlin.26Margolskee-21">[21]</a></sup> The average human detection threshold for sucrose is 10 millimoles per liter. For <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> it is 30 millimoles per liter, with a sweetness index of 0.3,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/5-Nitro-2-propoxyaniline" title="5-Nitro-2-propoxyaniline">5-Nitro-2-propoxyaniline</a> 0.002 millimoles per liter. “Natural” sweeteners such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saccharide" title="Saccharide">saccharides</a> activate the GPCR, which releases <a href="/wiki/Gustducin" title="Gustducin">gustducin</a>. The gustducin then activates the molecule <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenylate_cyclase" title="Adenylate cyclase">adenylate cyclase</a>, which catalyzes the production of the molecule <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_adenosine_monophosphate" title="Cyclic adenosine monophosphate">cAMP</a>, or adenosine 3', 5'-cyclic monophosphate. This molecule closes potassium ion channels, leading to depolarization and neurotransmitter release. Synthetic sweeteners such as <a href="/wiki/Saccharin" title="Saccharin">saccharin</a> activate different GPCRs and induce taste receptor cell depolarization by an alternate pathway.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sourness">Sourness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sourness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">"Sour" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sour_(disambiguation)" title="Sour (disambiguation)">Sour (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiktionary-logo-v2.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="391" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Wiktionary-logo-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-v2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Wiktionary-logo-v2.svg/60px-Wiktionary-logo-v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Wiktionary-logo-v2.svg/80px-Wiktionary-logo-v2.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Look up <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sour" title="wiktionary:sour">sour</a></b></i> in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signal_Transaction_of_Taste;_Sour_%26_Salty.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="512" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Sour_%26_Salty.svg/220px-Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Sour_%26_Salty.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Sour_%26_Salty.svg/330px-Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Sour_%26_Salty.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Sour_%26_Salty.svg/440px-Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Sour_%26_Salty.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signal_Transaction_of_Taste;_Sour_%26_Salty.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The diagram depicts the signal transduction pathway of the sour or salty taste. Object A is a taste bud, object B is a taste receptor cell within object A, and object C is the neuron attached to object B. I. Part I is the reception of hydrogen ions or sodium ions. 1. If the taste is sour, H+ ions, from an acidic substances, pass through their specific ion channel. Some can go through the Na+ channels. If the taste is salty Na+, sodium, molecules pass through the Na+ channels. Depolarization takes place II. Part II is the transduction pathway of the relay molecules.2. Cation, such as K+, channels are opened. III. Part III is the response of the cell. 3. An influx of Ca+ ions is activated.4. The Ca+ activates neurotransmitters. 5. A signal is sent to the neuron attached to the taste bud.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sourness is the taste that detects <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidity</a>. The sourness of substances is rated relative to dilute <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, which has a sourness index of 1. By comparison, <a href="/wiki/Tartaric_acid" title="Tartaric acid">tartaric acid</a> has a sourness index of 0.7, <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a> an index of 0.46, and <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> an index of 0.06.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaughlin.26Margolskee_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLaughlin.26Margolskee-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sour taste is detected by a small subset of cells that are distributed across all taste buds in the tongue. Sour taste cells can be identified by expression of the protein <a href="/wiki/PKD2L1" title="PKD2L1">PKD2L1</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> although this gene is not required for sour responses. There is evidence that the protons that are abundant in sour substances can directly enter the sour taste cells through apically located ion channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This transfer of positive charge into the cell can itself trigger an electrical response. It has also been proposed that weak acids such as acetic acid, which are not fully dissociated at physiological pH values, can penetrate taste cells and thereby elicit an electrical response. According to this mechanism, intracellular hydrogen ions inhibit potassium channels, which normally function to hyperpolarize the cell. By a combination of direct intake of hydrogen ions (which itself depolarizes the cell) and the inhibition of the hyperpolarizing channel, sourness causes the taste cell to fire action potentials and release neurotransmitter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most common food group that contains naturally sour foods is <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Lemon" title="Lemon">lemon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">orange</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamarind" title="Tamarind">tamarind</a>, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Melon" title="Melon">melon</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">Wine</a> also usually has a sour tinge to its flavor, and if not kept correctly, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a> can spoil and develop a sour taste. Children in the US and UK show a greater enjoyment of sour flavors than adults,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sour_sanding" title="Sour sanding">sour candy</a> is popular in North America<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Cry_Baby_(gum)" title="Cry Baby (gum)">Cry Babies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warheads_(candy)" title="Warheads (candy)">Warheads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lemon_drop_(candy)" title="Lemon drop (candy)">Lemon drops</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shock_Tarts" title="Shock Tarts">Shock Tarts</a> and sour versions of <a href="/wiki/Skittles_(confectionery)" title="Skittles (confectionery)">Skittles</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starburst_(candy)" title="Starburst (candy)">Starburst</a>. Many of these candies contain citric acid.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saltiness">Saltiness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Saltiness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">"Saltiness" redirects here. For the saltiness in the water, see <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">Salinity</a>.</div>
<p>The simplest receptor found in the mouth is the <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> (salt) receptor. Saltiness is a taste produced primarily by the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_ion" title="Sodium ion">sodium ions</a>. Other ions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" title="Alkali metals">alkali metals</a> group also taste salty, but the further from sodium, the less salty the sensation is. A <a href="/wiki/Sodium_channel" title="Sodium channel">sodium channel</a> in the taste cell wall allows sodium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cations</a> to enter the cell. This on its own depolarizes the cell, and opens <a href="/wiki/Voltage-dependent_calcium_channel" title="Voltage-dependent calcium channel">voltage-dependent calcium channels</a>, flooding the cell with positive calcium ions and leading to <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> release. This sodium channel is known as an <a href="/wiki/Epithelial_sodium_channel" title="Epithelial sodium channel">epithelial sodium channel</a> (ENaC) and is composed of three subunits. An ENaC can be blocked by the drug <a href="/wiki/Amiloride" title="Amiloride">amiloride</a> in many mammals, especially rats. The sensitivity of the salt taste to amiloride in humans, however, is much less pronounced, leading to conjecture that there may be additional receptor proteins besides ENaC to be discovered.</p>
<p>The size of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> ions most closely resemble those of sodium, and thus the saltiness is most similar. In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cesium" title="Cesium">cesium</a> ions are far larger, so their salty taste differs accordingly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The saltiness of substances is rated relative to sodium chloride (NaCl), which has an index of 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaughlin.26Margolskee_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-McLaughlin.26Margolskee-21">[21]</a></sup> Potassium, as <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> (KCl), is the principal ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Salt_substitute" title="Salt substitute">salt substitutes</a> and has a saltiness index of 0.6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaughlin.26Margolskee_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-McLaughlin.26Margolskee-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other <a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">monovalent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cations" title="Cations">cations</a>, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a>, NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>, and <a href="/wiki/Divalent" title="Divalent">divalent</a> cations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_earth_metal" title="Alkali earth metal">alkali earth metal</a> group of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, e.g. calcium, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, ions generally elicit a bitter rather than a salty taste even though they, too, can pass directly through ion channels in the tongue, generating an <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potential</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bitterness">Bitterness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bitterness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bitter_taste_evolution" title="Bitter taste evolution">Bitter taste evolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signal_Transaction_of_Taste;_Bitter.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="512" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Bitter.svg/220px-Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Bitter.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Bitter.svg/330px-Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Bitter.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Bitter.svg/440px-Signal_Transaction_of_Taste%3B_Bitter.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signal_Transaction_of_Taste;_Bitter.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The diagram depicted above shows the signal transduction pathway of the bitter taste. Bitter taste has many different receptors and signal transduction pathways. Bitter indicates poison to animals. It is most similar to sweet. Object A is a taste bud, object B is one taste cell, and object C is a neuron attached to object B. I. Part I is the reception of a molecule.1. A bitter substance such as quinine, is consumed and binds to G Protein-coupled receptors.II. Part II is the transduction pathway 2. Gustducin, a G protein second messenger, is activated. 3. Phosphodiesterase, an enzyme, is then activated. 4. Cyclic nucleotide, cNMP, is used, lowering the concentration 5. Channels such as the K+, potassium, channels, close.III. Part III is the response of the taste cell. 6. This leads to increased levels of Ca+. 7. The neurotransmitters are activated. 8. The signal is sent to the neuron.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bitterness is the most sensitive of the tastes, and many perceive it as unpleasant, sharp, or disagreeable, but it is sometimes desirable and intentionally added via various <a href="/wiki/Bittering_agent" title="Bittering agent">bittering agents</a>. Common bitter foods and beverages include <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>, unsweetened <a href="/wiki/Hot_chocolate" title="Hot chocolate">cocoa</a>, South American <a href="/wiki/Mate_(beverage)" title="Mate (beverage)">mate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitter_gourd" title="Bitter gourd">bitter gourd</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olive_(fruit)" title="Olive (fruit)">olives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">citrus peel</a>, many plants in the <a href="/wiki/Brassicaceae" title="Brassicaceae">Brassicaceae</a> family, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dandelion" title="Dandelion">dandelion</a> greens, wild <a href="/wiki/Chicory" title="Chicory">chicory</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Escarole" title="Escarole">escarole</a>. The ethanol in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverages" title="Alcoholic beverages">alcoholic beverages</a> tastes bitter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> as do the additional bitter ingredients found in some alcoholic beverages including <a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">hops</a> in <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and orange in <a href="/wiki/Bitters" title="Bitters">bitters</a>. <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">Quinine</a> is also known for its bitter taste and is found in <a href="/wiki/Tonic_water" title="Tonic water">tonic water</a>.</p>
<p>Bitterness is of interest to those who study <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, as well as various health researchers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psychologyofeating.26drinking_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-psychologyofeating.26drinking-28">[28]</a></sup> since a large number of natural bitter compounds are known to be toxic. The ability to detect bitter-tasting, toxic compounds at low thresholds is considered to provide an important protective function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-6"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psychologyofeating.26drinking_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-psychologyofeating.26drinking-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Plant leaves often contain toxic compounds, yet even amongst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leaf-eating" title="Leaf-eating">leaf-eating</a> primates, there is a tendency to prefer immature leaves, which tend to be higher in protein and lower in fiber and poisons than mature leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encylopediahumanevolution_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-encylopediahumanevolution-30">[30]</a></sup> Amongst humans, various <a href="/wiki/Food_processing" title="Food processing">food processing</a> techniques are used worldwide to detoxify otherwise inedible foods and make them palatable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Furthermore, the use of fire, changes in diet, and avoidance of toxins has led to neutral evolution in human bitter sensitivity. This has allowed several loss of function mutations that has led to a reduced sensory capacity towards bitterness in humans when compared to other species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The threshold for stimulation of bitter taste by quinine averages a concentration of 8 μ<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molarity" title="Molarity">M</a> (8 micromolar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-7"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup> The taste thresholds of other bitter substances are rated relative to quinine, which is thus given a reference index of 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-8"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaughlin.26Margolskee_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-McLaughlin.26Margolskee-21">[21]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Brucine" title="Brucine">Brucine</a> has an index of 11, is thus perceived as intensely more bitter than quinine, and is detected at a much lower solution threshold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-9"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup> The most bitter substance known is the synthetic chemical <a href="/wiki/Denatonium" title="Denatonium">denatonium</a>, which has an index of 1,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaughlin.26Margolskee_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-McLaughlin.26Margolskee-21">[21]</a></sup> It is used as an <a href="/wiki/Aversive_agent" title="Aversive agent">aversive agent</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Bitterant" title="Bitterant">bitterant</a>) that is added to toxic substances to prevent accidental ingestion. This was discovered in 1958 during research on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lignocaine" title="Lignocaine">lignocaine</a>, a local anesthetic, by <a href="/wiki/MacFarlan_Smith" title="MacFarlan Smith">MacFarlan Smith</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gorgie" title="Gorgie">Gorgie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Research has shown that TAS2Rs (taste receptors, type 2, also known as T2Rs) such as <a href="/wiki/TAS2R38" title="TAS2R38">TAS2R38</a> coupled to the <a href="/wiki/G_protein" title="G protein">G protein</a> <a href="/wiki/Gustducin" title="Gustducin">gustducin</a> are responsible for the human ability to taste bitter substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> They are identified not only by their ability to taste for certain "bitter" <a href="/wiki/Ligand_(biochemistry)" title="Ligand (biochemistry)">ligands</a>, but also by the morphology of the receptor itself (surface bound, monomeric).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The TAS2R family in humans is thought to comprise about 25 different taste receptors, some of which can recognize a wide variety of bitter-tasting compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Over 550 bitter-tasting compounds have been identified, on a <a href="/wiki/BitterDB" title="BitterDB">bitter database</a>, of which about 100 have been assigned to one or more specific receptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Recently it is speculated that the selective constraints on the TAS2R family have been weakened due to the relatively high rate of mutation and pseudogenization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Researchers use two synthetic substances, <a href="/wiki/Phenylthiocarbamide" title="Phenylthiocarbamide">phenylthiocarbamide</a> (PTC) and <a href="/wiki/Propylthiouracil" title="Propylthiouracil">6-n-propylthiouracil</a> (PROP) to study the <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> of bitter perception. These two substances taste bitter to some people, but are virtually tasteless to others. Among the tasters, some are so-called "<a href="/wiki/Supertaster" title="Supertaster">supertasters</a>" to whom PTC and PROP are extremely bitter. The variation in sensitivity is determined by two common alleles at the TAS2R38 locus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This genetic variation in the ability to taste a substance has been a source of great interest to those who study genetics.</p>
<p>Gustducin is made of three subunits. When it is activated by the GPCR, its subunits break apart and activate <a href="/wiki/Phosphodiesterase" title="Phosphodiesterase">phosphodiesterase</a>, a nearby enzyme, which in turn converts a precursor within the cell into a secondary messenger, which closes potassium ion channels.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Also, this secondary messenger can stimulate the <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">endoplasmic reticulum</a> to release Ca2+ which contributes to depolarization. This leads to a build-up of potassium ions in the cell, depolarization, and neurotransmitter release. It is also possible for some bitter tastants to interact directly with the G protein, because of a structural similarity to the relevant GPCR.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Umami">Umami</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Umami">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">Umami</a></div>
<p>Umami is an <a href="/wiki/Appetite" title="Appetite">appetitive</a> taste<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aa_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-aa-12">[12]</a></sup> and is described as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savoriness" title="Savoriness">savory</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umamiMW_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-umamiMW-40">[40]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meaty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umamiMW_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-umamiMW-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> It can be tasted in <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umami_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-umami-42">[42]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-times-43">[43]</a></sup> and is also found in many other fermented and aged foods. This taste is also present in tomatoes, grains, and beans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umami_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-umami-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanword</a> from <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> meaning "good flavor" or "good taste",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <i>umami</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span class="t_nihongo_kanji" lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">旨味</span><sup class="t_nihongo_help noprint"><a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets"><span class="t_nihongo_icon" style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; text-decoration: none; padding: 0 .1em;">?</span></a></sup>)</span> is considered fundamental to many <a href="/wiki/Eastern_world" title="Eastern world">Eastern</a> cuisines;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmin_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-jmin-45">[45]</a></sup> and other cuisines have long operated under principles that sought to combine foods to produce umami flavors, such as in the emphasis on veal stock by <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Escoffier" title="Auguste Escoffier">Auguste Escoffier</a>, the pre-eminent chef of 19th century French cuisine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and in the Romans' deliberate use of fermented fish sauce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> However, it was only recently recognized in modern science as a basic taste; well after the other basic tastes have been recognized by scientists, in part due to their correspondence with the four tastes of ancient Greek philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-times-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umamiinfo_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-umamiinfo-48">[48]</a></sup> Umami, or “scrumptiousness”, was first studied with the scientific method and identified by <a href="/wiki/Kikunae_Ikeda" title="Kikunae Ikeda">Kikunae Ikeda</a>, who began to analyze <a href="/wiki/Kombu" title="Kombu">kombu</a> in 1907, attempting to isolate its <a href="/wiki/Dashi" title="Dashi">dashi</a> taste. He isolated a substance he called ajinomoto, Japanese for “at the origin of flavor”. Later identified as the chemical <a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">monosodium glutamate</a> (MSG), and increasingly used independently as a food additive,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kean_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msgt_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-msgt-49">[49]</a></sup> it is a sodium salt that produces a strong umami taste, especially combined with foods rich in <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a> such as meats, fish, nuts, and mushrooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-times_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-times-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamaguchi.26Ninomiya_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yamaguchi.26Ninomiya-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some umami taste buds respond specifically to glutamate in the same way that "sweet" ones respond to sugar. Glutamate binds to a variant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G_protein_coupled_receptor" title="G protein coupled receptor">G protein coupled glutamate receptors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindemann_B_99.E2.80.93100_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lindemann_B_99.E2.80.93100-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaudhari_N.2C_Landin_AM.2C_Roper_SD_113.E2.80.939_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaudhari_N.2C_Landin_AM.2C_Roper_SD_113.E2.80.939-52">[52]</a></sup> It is thought that the amino acid L-glutamate bonds to a type of GPCR known as a metabotropic glutamate receptor (<a href="/wiki/Metabotropic_glutamate_receptor_4" title="Metabotropic glutamate receptor 4">mGluR4</a>). This causes the G-protein complex to activate a secondary receptor, which ultimately leads to neurotransmitter release. The intermediate steps are not known. (See <a href="/wiki/TAS1R1" title="TAS1R1">TAS1R1</a> and <a href="/wiki/TAS1R3" title="TAS1R3">TAS1R3</a> pages for a further explanation of the amino-acid taste receptor).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measuring_relative_tastes">Measuring relative tastes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Measuring relative tastes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Measuring the degree to which a substance presents one basic taste can be achieved in a subjective way by comparing its taste to a reference substance.</p>
<p><b>Sweetness</b> is subjectively measured by comparing the threshold values, or level at which the presence of a dilute substance can be detected by a human taster, of different sweet substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsai2007_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsai2007-53">[53]</a></sup> Substances are usually measured relative to <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walters_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walters-54">[54]</a></sup> which is usually given an arbitrary index of 1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JoestenEal2007_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-JoestenEal2007-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coultate_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coultate-56">[56]</a></sup> or 100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehta2005_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mehta2005-57">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">Fructose</a> is about 1.4 times sweeter than sucrose; <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, a sugar found in honey and vegetables, is about three-quarters as sweet; and <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a>, a milk sugar, is one-half as sweet.<sup class="reference" id="ref_bnone"><a href="#endnote_bnone">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsai2007_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsai2007-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <b>sourness</b> of a substance can be rated by comparing it to very dilute <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> (HCl).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyton.26Hall2006_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guyton.26Hall2006-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Relative <b>saltiness</b> can be rated by comparison to a dilute salt solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">Quinine</a>, a bitter medicinal found in <a href="/wiki/Tonic_water" title="Tonic water">tonic water</a>, can be used to subjectively rate the <b>bitterness</b> of a substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qn_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-qn-60">[60]</a></sup> Units of dilute quinine hydrochloride (1 g in 2000 mL of water) can be used to measure the threshold bitterness concentration, the level at which the presence of a dilute bitter substance can be detected by a human taster, of other compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qn_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-qn-60">[60]</a></sup> More formal chemical analysis, while possible, is difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qn_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-qn-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functional_structure">Functional structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Functional structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1402_The_Tongue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1875" data-file-width="2229" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/1402_The_Tongue.jpg/350px-1402_The_Tongue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/1402_The_Tongue.jpg/525px-1402_The_Tongue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/1402_The_Tongue.jpg/700px-1402_The_Tongue.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1402_The_Tongue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Taste buds and papillae of the tongue</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the human body a <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimulus</a> refers to a form of energy which elicits a physiological or psychological action or response. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_receptors" title="Sensory receptors">Sensory receptors</a> are the structures in the body which change the stimulus from one form of energy to another. This can mean changing the presence of a chemical, sound wave, source of heat, or touch to the skin into an electrical <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potential</a> which can be understood by the brain, the body’s control center. Sensory receptors are modified ends of sensory <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurons" title="Neurons">neurons</a>; modified to deal with specific types of stimulus, thus there are many different types of sensory receptors in the body. The neuron is the primary component of the nervous system, which transmits messages from sensory receptors all over the body.</p>
<p>Taste is a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemoreception" title="Chemoreception">chemoreception</a> which occurs in the specialised <a href="/wiki/Taste_receptor" title="Taste receptor">taste receptors</a> in the mouth. To date, there are five different types of taste receptors known: salt, sweet, sour, bitter, and umami. Each receptor has a different manner of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_transduction" title="Sensory transduction">sensory transduction</a>: that is, of detecting the presence of a certain compound and starting an action potential which alerts the brain. It is a matter of debate whether each taste cell is tuned to one specific tastant or to several; Smith and Margolskee claim that "gustatory neurons typically respond to more than one kind of stimulus, [a]lthough each neuron responds most strongly to one tastant". Researchers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe that the brain interprets complex tastes by examining patterns from a large set of neuron responses. This enables the body to make "keep or spit out" decisions when there is more than one tastant present. "No single neuron type alone is capable of discriminating among stimuli or different qualities, because a given cell can respond the same way to disparate stimuli."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As well, <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> is thought to act as an intermediary hormone which communicates with taste cells within a taste bud, mediating the signals being sent to the brain. Receptor molecules are found on the top of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microvilli" title="Microvilli">microvilli</a> of the taste cells.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Sweetness</dt>
</dl>
<p>Sweetness is produced by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a>, some proteins, and a few other substances.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is often connected to <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>, which contain a <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl_group" title="Carbonyl group">carbonyl group</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Sweetness is detected by a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G_protein-coupled_receptor" title="G protein-coupled receptor">G protein-coupled receptors</a> coupled to a <a href="/wiki/G_protein" title="G protein">G protein</a> that acts as an intermediary in the communication between taste bud and brain, <a href="/wiki/Gustducin" title="Gustducin">gustducin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> These receptors are T1R2+3 (heterodimer) and T1R3 (homodimer), which account for sweet sensing in humans and other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Sourness</dt>
</dl>
<p>Sourness is <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and, like salt, it is a taste sensed using <a href="/wiki/Ion_channel" title="Ion channel">ion channels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ion_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ion-65">[65]</a></sup> Undissociated acid diffuses across the plasma membrane of a presynaptic cell, where it dissociates in accordance with Le Chatelier's principle. The protons that are released then block potassium channels, which depolarise the cell and cause calcium influx. In addition, the taste receptor PKD2L1 has been found to be involved in tasting sour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceDaily24Aug06Sour_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceDaily24Aug06Sour-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Saltiness</dt>
</dl>
<p>Saltiness is a taste produced best by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">cations</a> (such as <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br/></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br/></span></span> or <span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br/></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ion_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-ion-65">[65]</a></sup> and is directly detected by cation influx into glial like cells via leak channels causing depolarisation of the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ion_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-ion-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other <a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">monovalent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cations" title="Cations">cations</a>, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Ammonium" title="Ammonium">ammonium</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">+<br/>
4</span></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Divalent" title="Divalent">divalent</a> cations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_earth_metal" title="Alkali earth metal">alkali earth metal</a> group of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, e.g., calcium, <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2+<br/></span></span>, ions, in general, elicit a bitter rather than a salty taste even though they, too, can pass directly through <a href="/wiki/Ion_channel" title="Ion channel">ion channels</a> in the tongue.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Bitterness</dt>
</dl>
<p>Research has shown that TAS2Rs (taste receptors, type 2, also known as T2Rs) such as <a href="/wiki/TAS2R38" title="TAS2R38">TAS2R38</a> are responsible for the human ability to taste bitter substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> They are identified not only by their ability to taste certain bitter ligands, but also by the morphology of the receptor itself (surface bound, monomeric).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Umami</dt>
</dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamic acid</a> is responsible for umami,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umami2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-umami2-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChandrashekarHoonEtal2006_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChandrashekarHoonEtal2006-70">[70]</a></sup> but some <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a> (<a href="/wiki/Inosinic_acid" title="Inosinic acid">inosinic acid</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmin_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-jmin-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umami3_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-umami3-71">[71]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guanylic_acid" title="Guanylic acid">guanylic acid</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umami2_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-umami2-69">[69]</a></sup>) can act as complements, enhancing the taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmin_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-jmin-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umami3_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-umami3-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Glutamic acid binds to a variant of the G protein-coupled receptor, producing an <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a> taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindemann_B_99.E2.80.93100_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lindemann_B_99.E2.80.93100-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaudhari_N.2C_Landin_AM.2C_Roper_SD_113.E2.80.939_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chaudhari_N.2C_Landin_AM.2C_Roper_SD_113.E2.80.939-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_sensations_and_transmission">Further sensations and transmission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Further sensations and transmission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The tongue can also feel other sensations not generally included in the basic tastes. These are largely detected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somatosensory" title="Somatosensory">somatosensory</a> system. In humans, the sense of taste is conveyed via three of the twelve cranial nerves. The <a href="/wiki/Facial_nerve" title="Facial nerve">facial nerve</a> (VII) carries taste sensations from the anterior two thirds of the <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Glossopharyngeal_nerve" title="Glossopharyngeal nerve">glossopharyngeal nerve</a> (IX) carries taste sensations from the posterior one third of the tongue while a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Vagus_nerve" title="Vagus nerve">vagus nerve</a> (X) carries some taste sensations from the back of the oral cavity.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Trigeminal_nerve" title="Trigeminal nerve">trigeminal nerve</a> (cranial nerve V) provides information concerning the general texture of food as well as the taste-related sensations of peppery or hot (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spices" title="Spices">spices</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pungency_.28also_spiciness_or_hotness.29">Pungency (also spiciness or hotness)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Pungency (also spiciness or hotness)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pungency" title="Pungency">Pungency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scoville_scale" title="Scoville scale">Scoville scale</a></div>
<p>Substances such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Capsaicin" title="Capsaicin">capsaicin</a> cause a burning sensation by inducing a trigeminal nerve reaction together with normal taste reception. The sensation of heat is caused by the food's activating nerves that express <a href="/wiki/TRPV1" title="TRPV1">TRPV1</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TRPA1" title="TRPA1">TRPA1</a> receptors. Some such plant-derived compounds that provide this sensation are capsaicin from <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili peppers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Piperine" title="Piperine">piperine</a> from <a href="/wiki/Black_pepper" title="Black pepper">black pepper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gingerol" title="Gingerol">gingerol</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ginger_root" title="Ginger root">ginger root</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allyl_isothiocyanate" title="Allyl isothiocyanate">allyl isothiocyanate</a> from <a href="/wiki/Horseradish" title="Horseradish">horseradish</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Pungency" title="Pungency">piquant</a> ("hot" or "spicy") sensation provided by such foods and spices plays an important role in a diverse range of cuisines across the world—especially in equatorial and sub-tropical climates, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Ethiopia" title="Cuisine of Ethiopia">Ethiopian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_cuisine" title="Peruvian cuisine">Peruvian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_cuisine" title="Hungarian cuisine">Hungarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_cuisine" title="Indian cuisine">Indian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Korea" title="Cuisine of Korea">Korean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_cuisine" title="Indonesian cuisine">Indonesian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Laos" title="Cuisine of Laos">Lao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_cuisine" title="Malaysian cuisine">Malaysian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexican_cuisine" title="Mexican cuisine">Mexican</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexican_cuisine" title="New Mexican cuisine">New Mexican</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singaporean_cuisine" title="Singaporean cuisine">Singaporean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southwest_China" title="Southwest China">Southwest Chinese</a> (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Szechuan_cuisine" title="Szechuan cuisine">Szechuan cuisine</a>), <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_cuisine" title="Vietnamese cuisine">Vietnamese</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Thailand" title="Cuisine of Thailand">Thai</a> cuisines.</p>
<p>This particular sensation, called <a href="/wiki/Chemesthesis" title="Chemesthesis">chemesthesis</a>, is not a taste in the technical sense, because the sensation does not arise from taste buds, and a different set of nerve fibers carry it to the brain. Foods like chili peppers activate nerve fibers directly; the sensation interpreted as "hot" results from the stimulation of somatosensory (pain/temperature) fibers on the tongue. Many parts of the body with exposed membranes but no taste sensors (such as the nasal cavity, under the fingernails, <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">surface of the eye</a> or a wound) produce a similar sensation of heat when exposed to hotness agents. <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a> countries within the sphere of, mainly, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_cuisine" title="Chinese cuisine">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_cuisine" title="Indian cuisine">Indian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese</a> cultural influence, often wrote of <a href="/wiki/Pungency" title="Pungency">pungency</a> as a fifth or sixth taste.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coolness">Coolness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Coolness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some substances activate cold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigeminal" title="Trigeminal">trigeminal</a> receptors even when not at low temperatures. This "fresh" or "minty" sensation can be tasted in <a href="/wiki/Peppermint" title="Peppermint">peppermint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spearmint" title="Spearmint">spearmint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Menthol" title="Menthol">menthol</a>, ethanol, and <a href="/wiki/Camphor" title="Camphor">camphor</a>. Caused by activation of the same mechanism that signals cold, <a href="/wiki/TRPM8" title="TRPM8">TRPM8</a> ion channels on <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">nerve cells</a>, unlike the actual change in temperature described for sugar substitutes, this coolness is only a perceived phenomenon.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbness">Numbness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Numbness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Both Chinese and <a href="/wiki/Toba_Batak_people" title="Toba Batak people">Batak Toba</a> cooking include the idea of 麻 (<i>má</i> or <i>mati rasa</i>), a tingling numbness caused by spices such as <a href="/wiki/Sichuan_pepper" title="Sichuan pepper">Sichuan pepper</a>. The cuisines of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> province in China and of the Indonesian province of <a href="/wiki/North_Sumatra" title="North Sumatra">North Sumatra</a> often combine this with <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili pepper</a> to produce a 麻辣 <i>málà</i>, "numbing-and-hot", or "mati rasa" flavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> These sensations although not taste fall into a category of <a href="/wiki/Chemesthesis" title="Chemesthesis">Chemesthesis</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astringency">Astringency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Astringency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some foods, such as unripe fruits, contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tannins" title="Tannins">tannins</a> or <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxalate" title="Calcium oxalate">calcium oxalate</a> that cause an astringent or puckering sensation of the mucous membrane of the mouth. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_wine" title="Red wine">red wine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhubarb" title="Rhubarb">rhubarb</a>, some fruits of the <a href="/wiki/Syzygium" title="Syzygium">Syzygium</a> genus, and unripe <a href="/wiki/Persimmon" title="Persimmon">persimmons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>.</p>
<p>Less exact terms for the astringent sensation are "dry", "rough", "harsh" (especially for wine), "tart" (normally referring to sourness), "rubbery", "hard" or "styptic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>When referring to wine, <i>dry</i> is the opposite of <i>sweet,</i> and does not refer to astringency. Wines that contain tannins and so cause an astringent sensation are not necessarily classified as "dry," and "dry" wines are not necessarily astringent.</p>
<p>In the Indian Ayurvedic tradition, one of the six tastes is astringency (<i>kasaaya</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinhala_Language" title="Sinhala Language">Sinhala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_English" title="Sri Lankan English">Sri Lankan English</a> it is referred to as <i>kahata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallicness">Metallicness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Metallicness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A metallic taste may be caused by food and drink, certain medicines or <a href="/wiki/Amalgam_(dentistry)" title="Amalgam (dentistry)">amalgam</a> dental fillings. It is generally considered an off flavor when present in food and drink. A metallic taste may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_cell" title="Galvanic cell">galvanic</a> reactions in the mouth. In the case where it is caused by dental work, the dissimilar metals used may produce a measurable current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Some artificial sweeteners are perceived to have a metallic taste, which is detected by the <a href="/wiki/TRPV1" title="TRPV1">TRPV1</a> receptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">Blood</a> is considered by many people to have a metallic taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> A metallic taste in the mouth is also a symptom of various medical conditions, in which case it may be classified under the symptoms <a href="/wiki/Dysgeusia" title="Dysgeusia">dysgeusia</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parageusia" title="Parageusia">parageusia</a>, referring to distortions of the sense of taste,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldstein_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldstein-80">[80]</a></sup> and can be caused by various kinds of medication, including <a href="/wiki/Saquinavir" title="Saquinavir">saquinavir</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldstein_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-goldstein-80">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Zonisamide" title="Zonisamide">zonisamide</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and occupational hazards, such as working with pesticides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calcium">Calcium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Calcium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The distinctive taste of chalk has been identified as the calcium component of that substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In 2008, geneticists discovered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CaSR" title="CaSR">CaSR</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcium_receptor" title="Calcium receptor">calcium receptor</a> on the tongues of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mice" title="Mice">mice</a>. The CaSR receptor is commonly found in the <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidneys" title="Kidneys">kidneys</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>. Along with the "sweet" T1R3 receptor, the CaSR receptor can detect calcium as a taste. Whether closely related genes in mice and humans means the phenomenon exists in humans as well is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceDaily21Aug08Calcium_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceDaily21Aug08Calcium-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
