<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fattiness_.28oleogustus.29">Fattiness (oleogustus)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Fattiness (oleogustus)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recent research reveals a potential <a href="/wiki/Taste_receptor" title="Taste receptor">taste receptor</a> called the <a href="/wiki/CD36" title="CD36">CD36 receptor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> CD36 was targeted as a possible lipid taste receptor because it binds to <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> molecules (more specifically, long-chain <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and it has been localized to <a href="/wiki/Taste_bud" title="Taste bud">taste bud</a> cells (specifically, the circumvallate and foliate <a href="/wiki/Lingual_papillae" title="Lingual papillae">papillae</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> There is a debate over whether we can truly taste fats, and supporters of our ability to taste free fatty acids (FFAs) have based the argument on a few main points: there is an evolutionary advantage to oral fat detection; a potential fat receptor has been located on taste bud cells; fatty acids evoke specific responses that activate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gustatory_system" title="Gustatory system">gustatory</a> neurons, similar to other currently accepted tastes; and, there is a physiological response to the presence of oral fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21557960_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21557960-91">[91]</a></sup> Although CD36 has been studied primarily in <a href="/wiki/House_mouse" title="House mouse">mice</a>, research examining human subjects' ability to taste fats found that those with high levels of CD36 <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expression</a> were more sensitive to tasting fat than were those with low levels of CD36 expression;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> this study points to a clear association between CD36 receptor quantity and the ability to taste fat.</p>
<p>Other possible fat taste receptors have been identified. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G_protein-coupled_receptor" title="G protein-coupled receptor">G protein-coupled receptors</a> <a href="/wiki/GPR120" title="GPR120">GPR120</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_fatty_acid_receptor_1" title="Free fatty acid receptor 1">GPR40</a> have been linked to fat taste, because their absence resulted in reduced preference to two types of fatty acid (<a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>), as well as decreased neuronal response to oral fatty acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monovalent cation channel <a href="/wiki/TRPM5" title="TRPM5">TRPM5</a> has been implicated in fattiness taste as well,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> but it is thought to be involved primarily in downstream processing of the taste rather than primary reception, as it is with other tastes such as bitter, sweet, and umami.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21557960_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21557960-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2015 study, proposed naming the taste of fat as "oleogustus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oleogustus_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-oleogustus-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The main form of fat that is commonly ingested is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triglycerides" title="Triglycerides">triglycerides</a>, which are composed of three fatty acids bound together. In this state, triglycerides are able to give fatty foods unique textures that are often described as creaminess. But this texture is not an actual taste. It is only during ingestion that the fatty acids that make up triglycerides are broken apart and the taste of fat is revealed. The taste is commonly related to other, more negative, tastes such as bitter and sour due to how unpleasant the taste is for humans. Richard Mattes, a co-author of the study, explained that low concentrations of these fatty acids can create an overall better flavor in a food, much like how small uses of bitterness can make certain foods more rounded. However, a high concentration of fatty acids in certain foods is generally considered inedible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purdue_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Purdue-96">[96]</a></sup> To demonstrate that individuals can distinguish oleogustus from other flavors, the researchers separated volunteers into groups and had them try samples that also contained the other basic tastes. Volunteers were able to separate the taste of fatty acids into their own category, with some overlap with umami samples, which the researchers hypothesized was due to poor familiarity with both. The researchers note that the usual "creaminess and viscosity we associate with fatty foods is largely due to triglycerides", unrelated to the taste; while the actual taste of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatty_acids" title="Fatty acids">fatty acids</a> is not pleasant. Mattes described the taste as "more of a warning system" that a certain food should not be eaten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oleogustus_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-oleogustus-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are few regularly consumed foods rich in oleogustus, due to the negative flavor that is evoked in large quantities. Foods whose flavor to which oleogustus makes a small contribution include olive oil and fresh butter, along with various kinds of vegetable and nut oils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heartiness_.28kokumi.29">Heartiness (<i>kokumi</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Heartiness (kokumi)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some Japanese researchers refer to the <i>kokumi</i> of foods. This sensation has also been described as mouthfulness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foodprot_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-foodprot-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:290</sup> and appears to be related to a number of <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Alpha_and_beta_carbon" title="Alpha and beta carbon">γ</a>-<a href="/wiki/Dextrorotation_and_levorotation" title="Dextrorotation and levorotation">L</a>-<a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamyl</a> peptides</span>, which activate a <a href="/wiki/Calcium-sensing_receptor" title="Calcium-sensing receptor">calcium-sensing receptor</a> which is also sensitive to <a href="/wiki/Glutathione" title="Glutathione">glutathione</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foodprot_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-foodprot-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature">Temperature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Temperature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Temperature can be an essential element of the taste experience. Food and drink that—in a given culture—is traditionally served hot is often considered distasteful if cold, and vice versa. For example, alcoholic beverages, with a few exceptions, are usually thought best when served at room temperature or chilled to varying degrees, but soups—again, with exceptions—are usually only eaten hot. A cultural example are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_drinks" title="Soft drinks">soft drinks</a>. In North America it is almost always preferred cold, regardless of season. In South America soda is almost exclusively consumed lukewarm in winter<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, though not in Brazil, where it is only consumed cold.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Starchiness">Starchiness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Starchiness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2016 study suggested that humans can taste <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> (specifically, a <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> <a href="/wiki/Oligomer" title="Oligomer">oligomer</a>) independently of other tastes such as sweetness. However, no specific chemical receptor has been yet been found for this taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nerve_supply_and_neural_connections">Nerve supply and neural connections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nerve supply and neural connections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comprehensive_List_of_Relevant_Pathways_for_the_Gustatory_System.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="888" data-file-width="1553" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Comprehensive_List_of_Relevant_Pathways_for_the_Gustatory_System.png/220px-Comprehensive_List_of_Relevant_Pathways_for_the_Gustatory_System.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Comprehensive_List_of_Relevant_Pathways_for_the_Gustatory_System.png/330px-Comprehensive_List_of_Relevant_Pathways_for_the_Gustatory_System.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Comprehensive_List_of_Relevant_Pathways_for_the_Gustatory_System.png/440px-Comprehensive_List_of_Relevant_Pathways_for_the_Gustatory_System.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comprehensive_List_of_Relevant_Pathways_for_the_Gustatory_System.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This diagram linearly (unless otherwise mentioned) tracks the projections of all known structures that allow for taste to their relevant endpoints in the human brain.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Glossopharyngeal_nerve#Overview_of_special_sensory_component" title="Glossopharyngeal nerve">glossopharyngeal nerve</a> innervates a third of the tongue including the circumvallate papillae. The <a href="/wiki/Facial_nerve" title="Facial nerve">facial nerve</a> innervates the other two thirds of the tongue and the <a href="/wiki/Cheek" title="Cheek">cheek</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Chorda_tympani" title="Chorda tympani">chorda tympani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pterygopalatine_ganglion" title="Pterygopalatine ganglion">pterygopalatine ganglia</a> are ganglia (one on each side) of the <a href="/wiki/Soft_palate" title="Soft palate">soft palate</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Greater_petrosal_nerve" title="Greater petrosal nerve">greater petrosal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lesser_palatine_nerve" title="Lesser palatine nerve">lesser palatine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zygomatic_nerve" title="Zygomatic nerve">zygomatic nerves</a> all synapse here. The greater petrosal, carries soft palate taste signals to the facial nerve. The lesser palatine sends signals to the <a href="/wiki/Nasal_cavity" title="Nasal cavity">nasal cavity</a>; which is why spicy foods cause nasal drip. The zygomatic sends signals to the <a href="/wiki/Lacrimal_nerve" title="Lacrimal nerve">lacrimal nerve</a> that activate the <a href="/wiki/Lacrimal_gland" title="Lacrimal gland">lacrimal gland</a>; which is the reason that spicy foods can cause tears. Both the lesser palatine and the zygomatic are <a href="/wiki/Maxillary_nerve" title="Maxillary nerve">maxillary nerves</a> (from the <a href="/wiki/Trigeminal_nerve" title="Trigeminal nerve">trigeminal nerve</a>).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Special_visceral_afferent" title="Special visceral afferent">special visceral afferents</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Vagus_nerve" title="Vagus nerve">vagus nerve</a> carry taste from the <a href="/wiki/Epiglottis" title="Epiglottis">epiglottal</a> region of the tongue.</p>
<p>The lingual nerve (trigeminal, not shown in diagram) is deeply interconnected with chorda tympani in that it provides all other sensory info from the ⅔ of the tongue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> This info is processed separately (nearby) in rostal lateral subdivision of nucleus of the solitary tract (NST).</p>
<p>NST receives input from the amygdala (regulates oculomotor nuclei output), bed nuclei of stria terminalis, hypothalamus, and prefrontal cortex. NST is the topographical map that processes gustatory and sensory (temp, texture, etc.) info.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reticular formation (includes Raphe nuclei responsible for serotonin production) is signaled to release serotonin during and after a meal to suppress appetite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Similarly, salivary nuclei are signaled to decrease saliva secretion.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hypoglossal_nerve" title="Hypoglossal nerve">Hypoglossal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thalamus" title="Thalamus">thalamic</a> connections aid in oral-related movements.</p>
<p>Hypothalamus connections hormonally regulate hunger and the digestive system.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Substantia_innominata" title="Substantia innominata">Substantia innominata</a> connects the thalamus, temporal lobe, and insula.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edinger%E2%80%93Westphal_nucleus" title="Edinger–Westphal nucleus">Edinger-Westphal nucleus</a> reacts to taste stimuli by dilating and constricting the pupils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spinal ganglion are involved in movement.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Operculum_(brain)" title="Operculum (brain)">frontal operculum</a> is speculated to be the memory and association hub for taste.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insula_cortex" title="Insula cortex">insula cortex</a> aids in swallowing and gastric motility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_concepts">Other concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supertasters">Supertasters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Supertasters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supertaster" title="Supertaster">Supertaster</a></div>
<p>A supertaster is a person whose sense of taste is significantly more sensitive than average. The cause of this heightened response is likely, at least in part, due to an increased number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungiform_papillae" title="Fungiform papillae">fungiform papillae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Studies have shown that supertasters require less fat and sugar in their food to get the same satisfying effects. However, contrary to what one might think, these people actually tend to consume more salt than the average person. This is due to their heightened sense of the taste of bitterness, and the presence of salt drowns out the taste of bitterness. (This also explains why supertasters prefer salted cheddar cheese over non-salted.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftertaste">Aftertaste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Aftertaste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aftertaste" title="Aftertaste">Aftertaste</a></div>
<p>Aftertastes arise after food has been swallowed. An aftertaste can differ from the food it follows. <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">Medicines</a> and tablets may also have a lingering aftertaste, as they can contain certain artificial flavor compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Aspartame" title="Aspartame">aspartame</a> (artificial sweetener).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquired_taste">Acquired taste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Acquired taste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Acquired_taste" title="Acquired taste">Acquired taste</a></div>
<p>An acquired taste often refers to an appreciation for a food or beverage that is unlikely to be enjoyed by a person who has not had substantial exposure to it, usually because of some unfamiliar aspect of the food or beverage, including a strong or strange odor, taste, or appearance.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_significance">Clinical significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Clinical significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Patients with <a href="/wiki/Addison%27s_disease" title="Addison's disease">Addison's disease</a>, pituitary insufficiency, or <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a> sometimes have a hyper-sensitivity to the five primary tastes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disorders_of_taste">Disorders of taste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Disorders of taste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ageusia" title="Ageusia">ageusia</a> (complete loss of taste)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hypogeusia" title="Hypogeusia">hypogeusia</a> (reduced sense of taste)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dysgeusia" title="Dysgeusia">dysgeusia</a> (distortion in sense of taste)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parageusia" title="Parageusia">parageusia</a> (persistent abnormal taste)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hypergeusia" title="Hypergeusia">hypergeusia</a> (abnormally heightened sense of taste)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Western_world" title="The Western world">the West</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> postulated in <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">c.</a> 350 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> that the two most basic tastes were sweet and bitter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> He was one of the first to develop a list of basic tastes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a>, an ancient <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> healing science, has its own tradition of basic tastes, comprising <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">sweet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salty</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sour" title="Sour">sour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pungency" title="Pungency">pungent</a>, bitter &amp; <a href="/wiki/Astringent" title="Astringent">astringent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly, the Ancient Chinese regarded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiciness" title="Spiciness">spiciness</a> as a basic taste.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptors</a> for the basic tastes of bitter, sweet and umami have been identified. They are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G_protein-coupled_receptor" title="G protein-coupled receptor">G protein-coupled receptors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The cells that detect sourness have been identified as a subpopulation that express the protein <a href="/wiki/PKD2L1" title="PKD2L1">PKD2L1</a>. The responses are mediated by an influx of protons into the cells but the receptor for sour is still unknown. The receptor for <a href="/wiki/Amiloride" title="Amiloride">amiloride</a>-sensitive attractive salty taste in mice has been shown to be a sodium channel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> There is some evidence for a sixth taste that senses fatty substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, researchers found bitter <a href="/wiki/Taste_receptor" title="Taste receptor">taste receptors</a> in lung tissue, which cause airways to relax when a bitter substance is encountered. They believe this mechanism is evolutionarily adaptive because it helps clear lung infections, but could also be exploited to treat <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="refbegin" style=""><b>a.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_anone"><a href="#ref_anone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> It has been known for some time that these categories may not be comprehensive. In Guyton's 1976 edition of <i>Textbook of Medical Physiology</i>, he wrote:
<blockquote>
<p>On the basis of physiologic studies, there are generally believed to be at least four <i>primary</i> sensations of taste: <i>sour</i>, <i>salty</i>, <i>sweet,</i> and <i>bitter</i>. Yet we know that a person can perceive literally hundreds of different tastes. These are all supposed to be combinations of the four primary sensations...However, there might be other less conspicuous classes or subclasses of primary sensations",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyton1976_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guyton1976-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><b>b.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_bnone"><a href="#ref_bnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> Some variation in values is not uncommon between various studies. Such variations may arise from a range of methodological variables, from sampling to analysis and interpretation. In fact there is a "plethora of methods"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macbeth.26MacClancy2004_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macbeth.26MacClancy2004-119">[119]</a></sup> Indeed, the taste index of 1, assigned to reference substances such as sucrose (for sweetness), hydrochloric acid (for sourness), quinine (for bitterness), and sodium chloride (for saltiness), is itself arbitrary for practical purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyton.26Hall2006_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guyton.26Hall2006-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some values, such as those for maltose and glucose, vary little. Others, such as aspartame and sodium saccharin, have much larger variation. Regardless of variation, the perceived intensity of substances relative to each reference substance remains consistent for taste ranking purposes. The indices table for McLaughlin &amp; Margolskee (1994) for example,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed_20-10"><a href="#cite_note-textbookofmedicalphysiology8thed-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLaughlin.26Margolskee1994_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLaughlin.26Margolskee1994-120">[120]</a></sup> is essentially the same as that of Svrivastava &amp; Rastogi (2003),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Svrivastava.26Rastogi2003_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Svrivastava.26Rastogi2003-121">[121]</a></sup> Guyton &amp; Hall (2006),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guyton.26Hall2006_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guyton.26Hall2006-58">[58]</a></sup> and Joesten <i>et al.</i> (2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JoestenEal2007_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-JoestenEal2007-55">[55]</a></sup> The rankings are all the same, with any differences, where they exist, being in the values assigned from the studies from which they derive.</p>
<p>As for the assignment of 1 or 100 to the index substances, this makes no difference to the rankings themselves, only to whether the values are displayed as whole numbers or decimal points. Glucose remains about three-quarters as sweet as sucrose whether displayed as 75 or 0.75.</p>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taste&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adjectival_form" title="Adjectival form">Adjectival form</a>: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gustatory" title="wikt:gustatory">gustatory</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-kids-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-kids_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-kids_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://kidshealth.org/kid/talk/qa/taste_buds.html" rel="nofollow">What Are Taste Buds?</a> kidshealth.org</span></li>
